<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_Japan" title="Religion in Japan">Religion in Japan</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Itsukushima_Shrine_Torii_Gate_(13890465459).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="4000" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Itsukushima_Shrine_Torii_Gate_%2813890465459%29.jpg/220px-Itsukushima_Shrine_Torii_Gate_%2813890465459%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Itsukushima_Shrine_Torii_Gate_%2813890465459%29.jpg/330px-Itsukushima_Shrine_Torii_Gate_%2813890465459%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Itsukushima_Shrine_Torii_Gate_%2813890465459%29.jpg/440px-Itsukushima_Shrine_Torii_Gate_%2813890465459%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Itsukushima_Shrine_Torii_Gate_(13890465459).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Torii" title="Torii">torii</a> of <a href="/wiki/Itsukushima_Shrine" title="Itsukushima Shrine">Itsukushima Shinto Shrine</a> near <a href="/wiki/Hiroshima" title="Hiroshima">Hiroshima</a></div></div></div>
<p>Japan's constitution guarantees full religious freedom.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Inoue2007_238-0"><a href="#cite_note-Inoue2007-238">[236]</a></sup> Upper estimates suggest that 84–96 percent of the Japanese population subscribe to <a href="/wiki/Shinto" title="Shinto">Shinto</a> as its indigenous religion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-239"><a href="#cite_note-239">[237]</a></sup> However, these estimates are based on people <a href="/wiki/Danka_system" title="Danka system">affiliated</a> with a temple, rather than the number of true believers. Many Japanese people practice both Shinto and <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a>; they can either identify with both religions or describe themselves as non-religious or spiritual.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-240"><a href="#cite_note-240">[238]</a></sup> The level of participation in religious ceremonies as a cultural tradition remains high, especially during <a href="/wiki/Japanese_festivals" title="Japanese festivals">festivals</a> and occasions such as the <a href="/wiki/Hatsum%C5%8Dde" title="Hatsumōde">first shrine visit</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Japanese_New_Year" title="Japanese New Year">New Year</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-241"><a href="#cite_note-241">[239]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Taoism" title="Taoism">Taoism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Confucianism" title="Confucianism">Confucianism</a> from China have also influenced Japanese beliefs and customs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-242"><a href="#cite_note-242">[240]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a> was first introduced into Japan by Jesuit missions starting in 1549. Today, 1%<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-243"><a href="#cite_note-243">[241]</a></sup> to 1.5% of the population are <a href="/wiki/Christianity_in_Japan" title="Christianity in Japan">Christians</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-244"><a href="#cite_note-244">[242]</a></sup> Throughout the latest century, Western customs originally related to Christianity (including <a href="/wiki/Marriage_in_Japan" title="Marriage in Japan">Western style weddings</a>, <a href="/wiki/Valentine%27s_Day" title="Valentine's Day">Valentine's Day</a> and <a href="/wiki/Christmas" title="Christmas">Christmas</a>) have become popular as secular customs among many Japanese.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-245"><a href="#cite_note-245">[243]</a></sup>
</p><p>About 90% of those practicing <a href="/wiki/Islam_in_Japan" title="Islam in Japan">Islam in Japan</a> are foreign-born migrants as of 2016<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Japan&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-246"><a href="#cite_note-246">[244]</a></sup> As of 2018<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Japan&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> there were an estimated 105 <a href="/wiki/Mosque" title="Mosque">mosques</a> and 200,000 Muslims in Japan, 43,000 of which were ethnically Japanese.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-247"><a href="#cite_note-247">[245]</a></sup> Other minority religions include <a href="/wiki/Hinduism_in_Japan" title="Hinduism in Japan">Hinduism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">Judaism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Bah%C3%A1%CA%BC%C3%AD_Faith_in_Japan" title="Baháʼí Faith in Japan">Baháʼí Faith</a>, as well as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Animist" title="Animist">animist</a> beliefs of the Ainu.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-248"><a href="#cite_note-248">[246]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Languages">Languages</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_Japan" title="Languages of Japan">Languages of Japan</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%E8%B1%9A%E9%AA%A8%E3%82%89%E3%83%BC%E3%82%81%E3%82%93_%E5%8D%9A%E5%A4%9A%E5%A4%A9%E7%A5%9E_%E3%81%84%E3%82%89%E3%81%A3%E3%81%97%E3%82%83%E3%81%84_2010_(5023366778).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3744" data-file-width="5616" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/%E8%B1%9A%E9%AA%A8%E3%82%89%E3%83%BC%E3%82%81%E3%82%93_%E5%8D%9A%E5%A4%9A%E5%A4%A9%E7%A5%9E_%E3%81%84%E3%82%89%E3%81%A3%E3%81%97%E3%82%83%E3%81%84_2010_%285023366778%29.jpg/220px-%E8%B1%9A%E9%AA%A8%E3%82%89%E3%83%BC%E3%82%81%E3%82%93_%E5%8D%9A%E5%A4%9A%E5%A4%A9%E7%A5%9E_%E3%81%84%E3%82%89%E3%81%A3%E3%81%97%E3%82%83%E3%81%84_2010_%285023366778%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/%E8%B1%9A%E9%AA%A8%E3%82%89%E3%83%BC%E3%82%81%E3%82%93_%E5%8D%9A%E5%A4%9A%E5%A4%A9%E7%A5%9E_%E3%81%84%E3%82%89%E3%81%A3%E3%81%97%E3%82%83%E3%81%84_2010_%285023366778%29.jpg/330px-%E8%B1%9A%E9%AA%A8%E3%82%89%E3%83%BC%E3%82%81%E3%82%93_%E5%8D%9A%E5%A4%9A%E5%A4%A9%E7%A5%9E_%E3%81%84%E3%82%89%E3%81%A3%E3%81%97%E3%82%83%E3%81%84_2010_%285023366778%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/%E8%B1%9A%E9%AA%A8%E3%82%89%E3%83%BC%E3%82%81%E3%82%93_%E5%8D%9A%E5%A4%9A%E5%A4%A9%E7%A5%9E_%E3%81%84%E3%82%89%E3%81%A3%E3%81%97%E3%82%83%E3%81%84_2010_%285023366778%29.jpg/440px-%E8%B1%9A%E9%AA%A8%E3%82%89%E3%83%BC%E3%82%81%E3%82%93_%E5%8D%9A%E5%A4%9A%E5%A4%A9%E7%A5%9E_%E3%81%84%E3%82%89%E3%81%A3%E3%81%97%E3%82%83%E3%81%84_2010_%285023366778%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%E8%B1%9A%E9%AA%A8%E3%82%89%E3%83%BC%E3%82%81%E3%82%93_%E5%8D%9A%E5%A4%9A%E5%A4%A9%E7%A5%9E_%E3%81%84%E3%82%89%E3%81%A3%E3%81%97%E3%82%83%E3%81%84_2010_(5023366778).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Kanji" title="Kanji">Kanji</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Hiragana" title="Hiragana">hiragana</a></i> signs</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Japanese_language" title="Japanese language">Japanese language</a> is Japan's <i>de facto</i> national language and the primary language of most people in the country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LangPolicy_249-0"><a href="#cite_note-LangPolicy-249">[247]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Japanese_writing_system" title="Japanese writing system">Japanese writing</a> uses <a href="/wiki/Kanji" title="Kanji">kanji</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_character" title="Chinese character">Chinese characters</a>) and two sets of <a href="/wiki/Kana" title="Kana">kana</a> (<a href="/wiki/Syllabary" title="Syllabary">syllabaries</a> based on <a href="/wiki/Cursive_script_(East_Asia)" title="Cursive script (East Asia)">cursive script</a> and <a href="/wiki/Radical_(Chinese_characters)" title="Radical (Chinese characters)">radicals</a> used by kanji), as well as the <a href="/wiki/Latin_alphabet" title="Latin alphabet">Latin alphabet</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arabic_numerals" title="Arabic numerals">Arabic numerals</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-250"><a href="#cite_note-250">[248]</a></sup> English has taken a major role in Japan as a business and international link language. As a result, the prevalence of English in the educational system has increased, with English classes becoming mandatory at all levels of the Japanese school system by 2020.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LangPolicy_249-1"><a href="#cite_note-LangPolicy-249">[247]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-251"><a href="#cite_note-251">[249]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Japanese_Sign_Language" title="Japanese Sign Language">Japanese Sign Language</a> is the primary <a href="/wiki/Sign_language" title="Sign language">sign language</a> used in Japan and has gained some official recognition, but its usage has been historically hindered by discriminatory policies and a lack of educational support.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LangPolicy_249-2"><a href="#cite_note-LangPolicy-249">[247]</a></sup>
</p><p>Besides Japanese, the <a href="/wiki/Ryukyuan_languages" title="Ryukyuan languages">Ryukyuan languages</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amami_language" title="Amami language">Amami</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kunigami_language" title="Kunigami language">Kunigami</a>, <a href="/wiki/Okinawan_language" title="Okinawan language">Okinawan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Miyakoan_language" title="Miyakoan language">Miyako</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yaeyama_language" title="Yaeyama language">Yaeyama</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yonaguni_language" title="Yonaguni language">Yonaguni</a>), part of the <a href="/wiki/Japonic_languages" title="Japonic languages">Japonic language family</a>, are spoken in the Ryukyu Islands chain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-252"><a href="#cite_note-252">[250]</a></sup> Few children learn these languages,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-253"><a href="#cite_note-253">[251]</a></sup> but local governments have sought to increase awareness of the traditional languages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-254"><a href="#cite_note-254">[252]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Ainu_language" title="Ainu language">Ainu language</a>, which is a <a href="/wiki/Language_isolate" title="Language isolate">language isolate</a>, is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moribund_language" title="Moribund language">moribund</a>, with only a few native speakers remaining as of 2014<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Japan&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-255"><a href="#cite_note-255">[253]</a></sup> Additionally, a number of other languages are taught and used by ethnic minorities, immigrant communities, and a growing number of foreign-language students, such as <a href="/wiki/Korean_language" title="Korean language">Korean</a> (including a distinct <a href="/wiki/Zainichi_Korean_language" title="Zainichi Korean language">Zainichi Korean dialect</a>), <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a> and <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_language" title="Portuguese language">Portuguese</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LangPolicy_249-3"><a href="#cite_note-LangPolicy-249">[247]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Education">Education</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Education_in_Japan" title="Education in Japan">Education in Japan</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tokyo_University_Entrance_Exam_Results_6.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1944" data-file-width="2592" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Tokyo_University_Entrance_Exam_Results_6.JPG/220px-Tokyo_University_Entrance_Exam_Results_6.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Tokyo_University_Entrance_Exam_Results_6.JPG/330px-Tokyo_University_Entrance_Exam_Results_6.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Tokyo_University_Entrance_Exam_Results_6.JPG/440px-Tokyo_University_Entrance_Exam_Results_6.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tokyo_University_Entrance_Exam_Results_6.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Students celebrating after the announcement of the results of the <a href="/wiki/Higher_education_in_Japan#University_entrance" title="Higher education in Japan">entrance examinations</a> to the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Tokyo" title="University of Tokyo">University of Tokyo</a></div></div></div>
<p>Since the 1947 <a href="/wiki/Fundamental_Law_of_Education" title="Fundamental Law of Education">Fundamental Law of Education</a>, compulsory education in Japan comprises <a href="/wiki/Elementary_schools_in_Japan" title="Elementary schools in Japan">elementary</a> and <a href="/wiki/Secondary_education_in_Japan#Junior_high_school" title="Secondary education in Japan">junior high school</a>, which together last for nine years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-256"><a href="#cite_note-256">[254]</a></sup> Almost all children continue their education at a three-year <a href="/wiki/Secondary_education_in_Japan" title="Secondary education in Japan">senior high school</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-257"><a href="#cite_note-257">[255]</a></sup> The two top-ranking universities in Japan are the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Tokyo" title="University of Tokyo">University of Tokyo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kyoto_University" title="Kyoto University">Kyoto University</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-258"><a href="#cite_note-258">[256]</a></sup> Starting in April 2016, various schools began the academic year with elementary school and junior high school integrated into one nine-year compulsory schooling program; <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Education,_Culture,_Sports,_Science_and_Technology" title="Ministry of Education, Culture, Sports, Science and Technology">MEXT</a> plans for this approach to be adopted nationwide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-259"><a href="#cite_note-259">[257]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Programme_for_International_Student_Assessment" title="Programme for International Student Assessment">Programme for International Student Assessment</a> (PISA) coordinated by the OECD ranks the knowledge and skills of Japanese 15-year-olds as the third best in the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OECD_Student_performance_260-0"><a href="#cite_note-OECD_Student_performance-260">[258]</a></sup> Japan is one of the top-performing <a href="/wiki/OECD" title="OECD">OECD</a> countries in reading literacy, math and sciences with the average student scoring 520 and has one of the world's highest-educated labor forces among OECD countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-261"><a href="#cite_note-261">[259]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OECD_Student_performance_260-1"><a href="#cite_note-OECD_Student_performance-260">[258]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-262"><a href="#cite_note-262">[260]</a></sup> It spent roughly 3.1% of its total GDP on education as of 2018<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Japan&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-263"><a href="#cite_note-263">[261]</a></sup> below the OECD average of 4.9%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-264"><a href="#cite_note-264">[262]</a></sup> In 2020, the country ranked third for the percentage of 25 to 64-year-olds that have attained tertiary education with 52.7%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OECD_GPS_Education_265-0"><a href="#cite_note-OECD_GPS_Education-265">[263]</a></sup> Approximately 61.5% of Japanese aged 25 to 34 have some form of tertiary education qualification, and bachelor's degrees are held by 31.3% of Japanese aged 25 to 64, the second most in the OECD after South Korea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OECD_GPS_Education_265-1"><a href="#cite_note-OECD_GPS_Education-265">[263]</a></sup> Japanese females are more highly educated compared to their male counterparts, as 59% of Japanese women possess a university degree, compared to 52% of Japanese men.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-266"><a href="#cite_note-266">[264]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Health">Health</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Health_in_Japan" title="Health in Japan">Health in Japan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Health_care_system_in_Japan" title="Health care system in Japan">Health care system in Japan</a></div>
<p>Health care in Japan is provided by national and local governments. Payment for personal medical services is offered through a universal health insurance system that provides relative equality of access, with fees set by a government committee. People without insurance through employers can participate in a national health insurance program administered by local governments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ikegami2014_267-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ikegami2014-267">[265]</a></sup> Since 1973, all elderly persons have been covered by government-sponsored insurance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-268"><a href="#cite_note-268">[266]</a></sup>
</p><p>Japan spent 10.74% of its total GDP on healthcare in 2019.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-269"><a href="#cite_note-269">[267]</a></sup> In 2020, the overall life expectancy in Japan at birth was 84.62 years (81.64 years for males and 87.74 years for females), the <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_life_expectancy" title="List of countries by life expectancy">highest in the world</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-270"><a href="#cite_note-270">[268]</a></sup> while it had a very low <a href="/wiki/Infant_mortality" title="Infant mortality">infant mortality rate</a> (2 per 1,000 <a href="/wiki/Live_birth_(human)" title="Live birth (human)">live births</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-271"><a href="#cite_note-271">[269]</a></sup> Since 1981, the principal cause of death in Japan is <a href="/wiki/Cancer" title="Cancer">cancer</a>, which accounted for 27% of the total deaths in 2018—followed by <a href="/wiki/Cardiovascular_disease" title="Cardiovascular disease">cardiovascular diseases</a>, which led to 15% of the deaths.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-272"><a href="#cite_note-272">[270]</a></sup> Japan has one of the world's <a href="/wiki/Suicide_in_Japan" title="Suicide in Japan">highest suicide rates</a>, which is considered a major social issue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-273"><a href="#cite_note-273">[271]</a></sup> Another significant public health issue is <a href="/wiki/Smoking_in_Japan" title="Smoking in Japan">smoking among Japanese men</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-274"><a href="#cite_note-274">[272]</a></sup> However, Japan has the lowest rate of heart disease in the OECD, and the lowest level of dementia among developed countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-275"><a href="#cite_note-275">[273]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">Culture</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Japan" title="Culture of Japan">Culture of Japan</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Japanese_popular_culture" title="Japanese popular culture">Japanese popular culture</a></div>
<p>Contemporary Japanese culture combines influences from Asia, Europe and North America.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Haffner_276-0"><a href="#cite_note-Haffner-276">[274]</a></sup> Traditional Japanese arts include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Japanese_handicrafts" title="Japanese handicrafts">crafts</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Japanese_pottery_and_porcelain" title="Japanese pottery and porcelain">ceramics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kimono" title="Kimono">textiles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Japanese_lacquerware" title="Japanese lacquerware">lacquerware</a>, <a href="/wiki/Japanese_sword" title="Japanese sword">swords</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Japanese_traditional_dolls" title="Japanese traditional dolls">dolls</a>; performances of <a href="/wiki/Bunraku" title="Bunraku">bunraku</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kabuki" title="Kabuki">kabuki</a>, <a href="/wiki/Noh" title="Noh">noh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Japanese_traditional_dance" title="Japanese traditional dance">dance</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Rakugo" title="Rakugo">rakugo</a>; and other practices, the <a href="/wiki/Japanese_tea_ceremony" title="Japanese tea ceremony">tea ceremony</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ikebana" title="Ikebana">ikebana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Japanese_martial_arts" title="Japanese martial arts">martial arts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Japanese_calligraphy" title="Japanese calligraphy">calligraphy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Origami" title="Origami">origami</a>, <a href="/wiki/Onsen" title="Onsen">onsen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Geisha" title="Geisha">Geisha</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_Japanese_games" title="List of Japanese games">games</a>. Japan has a developed system for the protection and promotion of both tangible and intangible <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cultural_Properties_of_Japan" title="Cultural Properties of Japan">Cultural Properties</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Treasures_of_Japan" title="National Treasures of Japan">National Treasures</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-277"><a href="#cite_note-277">[275]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/List_of_World_Heritage_Sites_in_Japan" title="List of World Heritage Sites in Japan">Twenty-two sites</a> have been inscribed on the UNESCO World Heritage List, eighteen of which are of cultural significance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-unesco1_102-1"><a href="#cite_note-unesco1-102">[100]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Art_and_architecture">Art and architecture</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Japanese_art" title="Japanese art">Japanese art</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Japanese_architecture" title="Japanese architecture">Japanese architecture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Japanese_garden" title="Japanese garden">Japanese garden</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Japanese_esthetics" title="Japanese esthetics">Japanese esthetics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Japanese_painting" title="Japanese painting">Japanese painting</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Japanese_sculpture" title="Japanese sculpture">Japanese sculpture</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/The_Great_Wave_off_Kanagawa.jpg/224px-The_Great_Wave_off_Kanagawa.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/The_Great_Wave_off_Kanagawa.jpg/298px-The_Great_Wave_off_Kanagawa.jpg 2x" width="149"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption"><a href="/wiki/Hokusai" title="Hokusai">Hokusai</a>'s 19th-century <a href="/wiki/Ukiyo-e" title="Ukiyo-e">ukiyo-e</a> <a href="/wiki/Woodblock_printing_in_Japan" title="Woodblock printing in Japan">woodblock print</a> <i><a href="/wiki/The_Great_Wave_off_Kanagawa" title="The Great Wave off Kanagawa">The Great Wave off Kanagawa</a></i></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:157px;max-width:157px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:103px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:150504_Ritsurin_Park_Takamatsu_Kagawa_pref_Japan01s3.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="4000" data-file-width="6000" decoding="async" height="103" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/150504_Ritsurin_Park_Takamatsu_Kagawa_pref_Japan01s3.jpg/155px-150504_Ritsurin_Park_Takamatsu_Kagawa_pref_Japan01s3.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/150504_Ritsurin_Park_Takamatsu_Kagawa_pref_Japan01s3.jpg/233px-150504_Ritsurin_Park_Takamatsu_Kagawa_pref_Japan01s3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/150504_Ritsurin_Park_Takamatsu_Kagawa_pref_Japan01s3.jpg/310px-150504_Ritsurin_Park_Takamatsu_Kagawa_pref_Japan01s3.jpg 2x" width="155"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption"><a href="/wiki/Ritsurin_Garden" title="Ritsurin Garden">Ritsurin Garden</a>, one of the most famous strolling gardens in Japan</div></div></div></div></div>
<p>The history of <a href="/wiki/Japanese_painting" title="Japanese painting">Japanese painting</a> exhibits synthesis and competition between native <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Japanese_esthetics" title="Japanese esthetics">Japanese esthetics</a> and imported ideas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated3_278-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated3-278">[276]</a></sup> The interaction between Japanese and European art has been significant: for example <a href="/wiki/Ukiyo-e" title="Ukiyo-e">ukiyo-e</a> prints, which began to be exported in the 19th century in the movement known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Japonism" title="Japonism">Japonism</a>, had a significant influence on the development of modern art in the West, most notably on <a href="/wiki/Post-Impressionism" title="Post-Impressionism">post-Impressionism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated3_278-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated3-278">[276]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Japanese_architecture" title="Japanese architecture">Japanese architecture</a> is a combination between local and other influences. It has traditionally been typified by wooden or mud plaster structures, elevated slightly off the ground, with tiled or thatched roofs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-279"><a href="#cite_note-279">[277]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Ise_Grand_Shrine" title="Ise Grand Shrine">Shrines of Ise</a> have been celebrated as the prototype of Japanese architecture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-280"><a href="#cite_note-280">[278]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Minka" title="Minka">Traditional housing</a> and many <a href="/wiki/Japanese_Buddhist_architecture" title="Japanese Buddhist architecture">temple buildings</a> see the use of <a href="/wiki/Tatami" title="Tatami">tatami</a> mats and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sh%C5%8Dji" title="Shōji">sliding doors</a> that break down the distinction between rooms and indoor and outdoor space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-281"><a href="#cite_note-281">[279]</a></sup> Since the 19th century, Japan has incorporated much of Western <a href="/wiki/Modern_architecture" title="Modern architecture">modern architecture</a> into construction and design.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-282"><a href="#cite_note-282">[280]</a></sup> It was not until after World War II that Japanese architects made an impression on the international scene, firstly with the work of architects like <a href="/wiki/Kenz%C5%8D_Tange" title="Kenzō Tange">Kenzō Tange</a> and then with movements like <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metabolist_Movement" title="Metabolist Movement">Metabolism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-283"><a href="#cite_note-283">[281]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Literature_and_philosophy">Literature and philosophy</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Japanese_literature" title="Japanese literature">Japanese literature</a>, <a href="/wiki/Japanese_poetry" title="Japanese poetry">Japanese poetry</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Japanese_philosophy" title="Japanese philosophy">Japanese philosophy</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Genji_emaki_01003_001.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="2142" decoding="async" height="103" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Genji_emaki_01003_001.jpg/220px-Genji_emaki_01003_001.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Genji_emaki_01003_001.jpg/330px-Genji_emaki_01003_001.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Genji_emaki_01003_001.jpg/440px-Genji_emaki_01003_001.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Genji_emaki_01003_001.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>12th-century <a href="/wiki/Genji_Monogatari_Emaki" title="Genji Monogatari Emaki">illustrated handscroll</a> of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Tale_of_Genji" title="The Tale of Genji">The Tale of Genji</a></i>, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Treasures_of_Japan" title="National Treasures of Japan">National Treasure</a></div></div></div>
<p>The earliest works of Japanese literature include the <i><a href="/wiki/Kojiki" title="Kojiki">Kojiki</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Nihon_Shoki" title="Nihon Shoki">Nihon Shoki</a></i> chronicles and the <i><a href="/wiki/Man%27y%C5%8Dsh%C5%AB" title="Man'yōshū">Man'yōshū</a></i> <a href="/wiki/List_of_Japanese_poetry_anthologies" title="List of Japanese poetry anthologies">poetry anthology</a>, all from the 8th century and written in Chinese characters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-284"><a href="#cite_note-284">[282]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-285"><a href="#cite_note-285">[283]</a></sup> In the early Heian period, the system of <a href="/wiki/Phonogram_(linguistics)" title="Phonogram (linguistics)">phonograms</a> known as <i>kana</i> (<a href="/wiki/Hiragana" title="Hiragana">hiragana</a> and <a href="/wiki/Katakana" title="Katakana">katakana</a>) was developed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-286"><a href="#cite_note-286">[284]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/The_Tale_of_the_Bamboo_Cutter" title="The Tale of the Bamboo Cutter">The Tale of the Bamboo Cutter</a></i> is considered the oldest extant Japanese narrative.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-287"><a href="#cite_note-287">[285]</a></sup> An account of court life is given in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Pillow_Book" title="The Pillow Book">The Pillow Book</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Sei_Sh%C5%8Dnagon" title="Sei Shōnagon">Sei Shōnagon</a>, while <i><a href="/wiki/The_Tale_of_Genji" title="The Tale of Genji">The Tale of Genji</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Murasaki_Shikibu" title="Murasaki Shikibu">Murasaki Shikibu</a> is often described as the world's first novel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-288"><a href="#cite_note-288">[286]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-289"><a href="#cite_note-289">[287]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the Edo period, the <a href="/wiki/Ch%C5%8Dnin" title="Chōnin">chōnin</a> ("townspeople") overtook the samurai aristocracy as producers and consumers of literature. The popularity of the works of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saikaku" title="Saikaku">Saikaku</a>, for example, reveals this change in readership and authorship, while <a href="/wiki/Matsuo_Bash%C5%8D" title="Matsuo Bashō">Bashō</a> revivified the poetic tradition of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kokinsh%C5%AB" title="Kokinshū">Kokinshū</a> with his <a href="/wiki/Haikai" title="Haikai">haikai</a> (<a href="/wiki/Haiku" title="Haiku">haiku</a>) and wrote the poetic travelogue <i><a href="/wiki/Oku_no_Hosomichi" title="Oku no Hosomichi">Oku no Hosomichi</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-290"><a href="#cite_note-290">[288]</a></sup> The Meiji era saw the decline of traditional literary forms as Japanese literature integrated Western influences. <a href="/wiki/Natsume_S%C5%8Dseki" title="Natsume Sōseki">Natsume Sōseki</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mori_%C5%8Cgai" title="Mori Ōgai">Mori Ōgai</a> were significant novelists in the early 20th century, followed by <a href="/wiki/Ry%C5%ABnosuke_Akutagawa" title="Ryūnosuke Akutagawa">Ryūnosuke Akutagawa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jun%27ichir%C5%8D_Tanizaki" title="Jun'ichirō Tanizaki">Jun'ichirō Tanizaki</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kaf%C5%AB_Nagai" title="Kafū Nagai">Kafū Nagai</a> and, more recently, <a href="/wiki/Haruki_Murakami" title="Haruki Murakami">Haruki Murakami</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kenji_Nakagami" title="Kenji Nakagami">Kenji Nakagami</a>. Japan has two <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Literature" title="Nobel Prize in Literature">Nobel Prize-winning</a> authors – <a href="/wiki/Yasunari_Kawabata" title="Yasunari Kawabata">Yasunari Kawabata</a> (1968) and <a href="/wiki/Kenzabur%C5%8D_%C5%8Ce" title="Kenzaburō Ōe">Kenzaburō Ōe</a> (1994).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-291"><a href="#cite_note-291">[289]</a></sup>
</p><p>Japanese philosophy has historically been a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Information_fusion" title="Information fusion">fusion</a> of both foreign, particularly <a href="/wiki/Chinese_philosophy" title="Chinese philosophy">Chinese</a> and <a href="/wiki/Western_philosophy" title="Western philosophy">Western</a>, and uniquely Japanese elements. In its literary forms, Japanese philosophy began about fourteen centuries ago. Confucian ideals remain evident in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Japanese_society" title="Japanese society">Japanese concept of society</a> and the self, and in the organization of the government and the structure of society.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Japanese_Confucian_Philosophy_292-0"><a href="#cite_note-Japanese_Confucian_Philosophy-292">[290]</a></sup> Buddhism has profoundly impacted Japanese psychology, metaphysics, and esthetics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-293"><a href="#cite_note-293">[291]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Performing_arts">Performing arts</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Music_of_Japan" title="Music of Japan">Music of Japan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Theatre_of_Japan" title="Theatre of Japan">Theatre of Japan</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%E6%98%A5%E6%97%A5%E7%A5%9E%E7%A4%BE%E3%83%BC%E7%AF%A0%E5%B1%B1%E3%83%BC%E7%BF%81%E5%A5%89%E7%B4%8DP1011774.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="960" data-file-width="1280" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/%E6%98%A5%E6%97%A5%E7%A5%9E%E7%A4%BE%E3%83%BC%E7%AF%A0%E5%B1%B1%E3%83%BC%E7%BF%81%E5%A5%89%E7%B4%8DP1011774.jpg/220px-%E6%98%A5%E6%97%A5%E7%A5%9E%E7%A4%BE%E3%83%BC%E7%AF%A0%E5%B1%B1%E3%83%BC%E7%BF%81%E5%A5%89%E7%B4%8DP1011774.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/%E6%98%A5%E6%97%A5%E7%A5%9E%E7%A4%BE%E3%83%BC%E7%AF%A0%E5%B1%B1%E3%83%BC%E7%BF%81%E5%A5%89%E7%B4%8DP1011774.jpg/330px-%E6%98%A5%E6%97%A5%E7%A5%9E%E7%A4%BE%E3%83%BC%E7%AF%A0%E5%B1%B1%E3%83%BC%E7%BF%81%E5%A5%89%E7%B4%8DP1011774.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/%E6%98%A5%E6%97%A5%E7%A5%9E%E7%A4%BE%E3%83%BC%E7%AF%A0%E5%B1%B1%E3%83%BC%E7%BF%81%E5%A5%89%E7%B4%8DP1011774.jpg/440px-%E6%98%A5%E6%97%A5%E7%A5%9E%E7%A4%BE%E3%83%BC%E7%AF%A0%E5%B1%B1%E3%83%BC%E7%BF%81%E5%A5%89%E7%B4%8DP1011774.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%E6%98%A5%E6%97%A5%E7%A5%9E%E7%A4%BE%E3%83%BC%E7%AF%A0%E5%B1%B1%E3%83%BC%E7%BF%81%E5%A5%89%E7%B4%8DP1011774.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Noh" title="Noh">Noh</a></i> performance at a Shinto shrine</div></div></div>
<p>Japanese music is eclectic and diverse. Many <a href="/wiki/Traditional_Japanese_musical_instruments" title="Traditional Japanese musical instruments">instruments</a>, such as the <a href="/wiki/Koto_(instrument)" title="Koto (instrument)">koto</a>, were introduced in the 9th and 10th centuries. The popular <a href="/wiki/Music_of_Japan#Folk_music" title="Music of Japan">folk music</a>, with the guitar-like <a href="/wiki/Shamisen" title="Shamisen">shamisen</a>, dates from the 16th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-294"><a href="#cite_note-294">[292]</a></sup> Western classical music, introduced in the late 19th century, forms an integral part of Japanese culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-295"><a href="#cite_note-295">[293]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Taiko#Kumi-daiko" title="Taiko">Kumi-daiko</a> (ensemble drumming) was developed in postwar Japan and became very popular in North America.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-296"><a href="#cite_note-296">[294]</a></sup> Popular music in post-war Japan has been heavily influenced by American and European trends, which has led to the evolution of <a href="/wiki/J-pop" title="J-pop">J-pop</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-297"><a href="#cite_note-297">[295]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Karaoke" title="Karaoke">Karaoke</a> is a significant cultural activity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-298"><a href="#cite_note-298">[296]</a></sup>
</p><p>The four traditional theaters from Japan are <i><a href="/wiki/Noh" title="Noh">noh</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Ky%C5%8Dgen" title="Kyōgen">kyōgen</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Kabuki" title="Kabuki">kabuki</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Bunraku" title="Bunraku">bunraku</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-299"><a href="#cite_note-299">[297]</a></sup> Noh is one of the oldest continuous theater traditions in the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-300"><a href="#cite_note-300">[298]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Holidays">Holidays</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Public_holidays_in_Japan" title="Public holidays in Japan">Public holidays in Japan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Japanese_festivals" title="Japanese festivals">Japanese festivals</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Young_ladies_at_Harajuku.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="412" data-file-width="674" decoding="async" height="134" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Young_ladies_at_Harajuku.jpg/220px-Young_ladies_at_Harajuku.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Young_ladies_at_Harajuku.jpg/330px-Young_ladies_at_Harajuku.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Young_ladies_at_Harajuku.jpg/440px-Young_ladies_at_Harajuku.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Young_ladies_at_Harajuku.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Young ladies celebrate <a href="/wiki/Coming_of_Age_Day" title="Coming of Age Day">Coming of Age Day</a><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">成人の日</span></span>, <span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn">Seijin no Hi</i></span><span style="margin-left:.09em">)</span></span> in <a href="/wiki/Harajuku" title="Harajuku">Harajuku</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tokyo" title="Tokyo">Tokyo</a></div></div></div>
<p>Officially, Japan has 16 national, government-recognized holidays. Public holidays in Japan are regulated by the Public Holiday Law<span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">国民の祝日に関する法律</span></span>, <span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn">Kokumin no Shukujitsu ni Kansuru Hōritsu</i></span>)</span> of 1948.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-301"><a href="#cite_note-301">[299]</a></sup> Beginning in 2000, Japan implemented the <a href="/wiki/Happy_Monday_System" title="Happy Monday System">Happy Monday System</a>, which moved a number of national holidays to Monday in order to obtain a long weekend.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-302"><a href="#cite_note-302">[300]</a></sup> The national holidays in Japan are <a href="/wiki/Japanese_New_Year" title="Japanese New Year">New Year's Day</a> on January 1, <a href="/wiki/Coming_of_Age_Day" title="Coming of Age Day">Coming of Age Day</a> on the second Monday of January, <a href="/wiki/National_Foundation_Day" title="National Foundation Day">National Foundation Day</a> on February 11, <a href="/wiki/The_Emperor%27s_Birthday" title="The Emperor's Birthday">The Emperor's Birthday</a> on February 23, <a href="/wiki/Vernal_Equinox_Day" title="Vernal Equinox Day">Vernal Equinox Day</a> on March 20 or 21, <a href="/wiki/Sh%C5%8Dwa_Day" title="Shōwa Day">Shōwa Day</a> on April 29, <a href="/wiki/Constitution_Memorial_Day" title="Constitution Memorial Day">Constitution Memorial Day</a> on May 3, <a href="/wiki/Greenery_Day" title="Greenery Day">Greenery Day</a> on May 4, <a href="/wiki/Children%27s_Day_(Japan)" title="Children's Day (Japan)">Children's Day</a> on May 5, <a href="/wiki/Marine_Day" title="Marine Day">Marine Day</a> on the third Monday of July, <a href="/wiki/Mountain_Day" title="Mountain Day">Mountain Day</a> on August 11, <a href="/wiki/Respect_for_the_Aged_Day" title="Respect for the Aged Day">Respect for the Aged Day</a> on the third Monday of September, <a href="/wiki/September_equinox" title="September equinox">Autumnal Equinox</a> on September 23 or 24, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Health_and_Sports_Day" title="Health and Sports Day">Health and Sports Day</a> on the second Monday of October, <a href="/wiki/Culture_Day" title="Culture Day">Culture Day</a> on November 3, and <a href="/wiki/Labor_Thanksgiving_Day" title="Labor Thanksgiving Day">Labor Thanksgiving Day</a> on November 23.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-303"><a href="#cite_note-303">[301]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cuisine">Cuisine</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Japanese_cuisine" title="Japanese cuisine">Japanese cuisine</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:East_West_sushi_01.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4492" data-file-width="6798" decoding="async" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/East_West_sushi_01.jpg/220px-East_West_sushi_01.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/East_West_sushi_01.jpg/330px-East_West_sushi_01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/East_West_sushi_01.jpg/440px-East_West_sushi_01.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:East_West_sushi_01.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A plate of <i><a href="/wiki/Sushi" title="Sushi">nigiri-zushi</a></i></div></div></div>
<p>Japanese cuisine offers a vast array of <a href="/wiki/Japanese_regional_cuisine" title="Japanese regional cuisine">regional specialties</a> that use traditional recipes and local ingredients.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-304"><a href="#cite_note-304">[302]</a></sup> Seafood and <a href="/wiki/Japanese_rice" title="Japanese rice">Japanese rice</a> or <a href="/wiki/Japanese_noodles" title="Japanese noodles">noodles</a> are traditional staples.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-305"><a href="#cite_note-305">[303]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Japanese_curry" title="Japanese curry">Japanese curry</a>, since its introduction to Japan from <a href="/wiki/British_Raj" title="British Raj">British India</a>, is so widely consumed that it can be termed a <a href="/wiki/National_dish" title="National dish">national dish</a>, alongside <a href="/wiki/Ramen" title="Ramen">ramen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sushi" title="Sushi">sushi</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-306"><a href="#cite_note-306">[304]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-307"><a href="#cite_note-307">[305]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-308"><a href="#cite_note-308">[306]</a></sup> Traditional Japanese sweets are known as <i>wagashi</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goldstein2015_309-0"><a href="#cite_note-Goldstein2015-309">[307]</a></sup> Ingredients such as <a href="/wiki/Red_bean_paste" title="Red bean paste">red bean paste</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mochi" title="Mochi">mochi</a> are used. More modern-day tastes includes <a href="/wiki/Green_tea_ice_cream" title="Green tea ice cream">green tea ice cream</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FujitaStallings2008_310-0"><a href="#cite_note-FujitaStallings2008-310">[308]</a></sup>
</p><p>Popular Japanese beverages include <a href="/wiki/Sake" title="Sake">sake</a>, which is a brewed rice beverage that typically contains 14–17% alcohol and is made by multiple fermentation of rice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Batt2014_311-0"><a href="#cite_note-Batt2014-311">[309]</a></sup> Beer has been brewed in Japan since the late 17th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BoultonQuain2013_312-0"><a href="#cite_note-BoultonQuain2013-312">[310]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Green_tea" title="Green tea">Green tea</a> is produced in Japan and prepared in forms such as <a href="/wiki/Matcha" title="Matcha">matcha</a>, used in the <a href="/wiki/Japanese_tea_ceremony" title="Japanese tea ceremony">Japanese tea ceremony</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-313"><a href="#cite_note-313">[311]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Media">Media</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Cinema_of_Japan" title="Cinema of Japan">Cinema of Japan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Manga" title="Manga">Manga</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anime" title="Anime">Anime</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Media_of_Japan" title="Media of Japan">Media of Japan</a></div>
<p>According to the 2015 NHK survey on television viewing in Japan, 79 percent of Japanese watch television daily.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-314"><a href="#cite_note-314">[312]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Japanese_television_drama" title="Japanese television drama">Japanese television dramas</a> are viewed both within Japan and internationally;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-315"><a href="#cite_note-315">[313]</a></sup> other popular shows are in the genres of <a href="/wiki/Japanese_variety_show" title="Japanese variety show">variety shows</a>, comedy, and news programs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-316"><a href="#cite_note-316">[314]</a></sup> Many Japanese <a href="/wiki/Media_franchise" title="Media franchise">media franchises</a> such as <i><a href="/wiki/Dragon_Ball" title="Dragon Ball">Dragon Ball</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/One_Piece" title="One Piece">One Piece</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Naruto" title="Naruto">Naruto</a></i> have gained considerable global popularity and are among the world's <a href="/wiki/List_of_highest-grossing_media_franchises" title="List of highest-grossing media franchises">highest-grossing media franchises</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/Pok%C3%A9mon" title="Pokémon">Pokémon</a></i> in particular is estimated to be the highest-grossing media franchise of all time. <a href="/wiki/Japanese_newspapers" title="Japanese newspapers">Japanese newspapers</a> are among the most circulated in the world as of 2016<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Japan&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-317"><a href="#cite_note-317">[315]</a></sup>
</p><p>Japan has one of the oldest and largest film industries globally.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-318"><a href="#cite_note-318">[316]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ishir%C5%8D_Honda" title="Ishirō Honda">Ishirō Honda</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Godzilla_(1954_film)" title="Godzilla (1954 film)">Godzilla</a></i> became an international icon of Japan and spawned an entire subgenre of <i><a href="/wiki/Kaiju" title="Kaiju">kaiju</a></i> films, as well as the longest-running film franchise in history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-319"><a href="#cite_note-319">[317]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-320"><a href="#cite_note-320">[318]</a></sup> Japanese comics, known as <a href="/wiki/Manga" title="Manga">manga</a>, developed in the mid-20th century and have become popular <a href="/wiki/Manga_outside_Japan" title="Manga outside Japan">worldwide</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kinko_Ito_456–475_321-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kinko_Ito_456–475-321">[319]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-322"><a href="#cite_note-322">[320]</a></sup> A large number of <a href="/wiki/List_of_best-selling_manga" title="List of best-selling manga">manga series</a> have become some of the <a href="/wiki/List_of_best-selling_comic_series" title="List of best-selling comic series">best-selling comics series of all time</a>, rivalling the <a href="/wiki/American_comic_book" title="American comic book">American comics industry</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-323"><a href="#cite_note-323">[321]</a></sup> Japanese animated films and television series, known as <a href="/wiki/Anime" title="Anime">anime</a>, were largely influenced by Japanese manga and have become highly popular internationally.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-324"><a href="#cite_note-324">[322]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-325"><a href="#cite_note-325">[323]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sports">Sports</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sport_in_Japan" title="Sport in Japan">Sport in Japan</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Aki_basho_dohy%C5%8D-iri_on_Sept._28_2014.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4016" data-file-width="6016" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Aki_basho_dohy%C5%8D-iri_on_Sept._28_2014.jpg/220px-Aki_basho_dohy%C5%8D-iri_on_Sept._28_2014.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Aki_basho_dohy%C5%8D-iri_on_Sept._28_2014.jpg/330px-Aki_basho_dohy%C5%8D-iri_on_Sept._28_2014.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Aki_basho_dohy%C5%8D-iri_on_Sept._28_2014.jpg/440px-Aki_basho_dohy%C5%8D-iri_on_Sept._28_2014.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Aki_basho_dohy%C5%8D-iri_on_Sept._28_2014.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Sumo" title="Sumo">Sumo</a> wrestlers form around the referee during the ring-entering ceremony</div></div></div>
<p>Traditionally, <a href="/wiki/Sumo" title="Sumo">sumo</a> is considered Japan's national sport.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-326"><a href="#cite_note-326">[324]</a></sup> Japanese martial arts such as <a href="/wiki/Judo" title="Judo">judo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kendo" title="Kendo">kendo</a> are taught as part of the compulsory junior high school curriculum.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-327"><a href="#cite_note-327">[325]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Baseball_in_Japan" title="Baseball in Japan">Baseball</a> is the most popular spectator sport in the country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-328"><a href="#cite_note-328">[326]</a></sup> Japan's top professional league, <a href="/wiki/Nippon_Professional_Baseball" title="Nippon Professional Baseball">Nippon Professional Baseball</a>, was established in 1936.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-329"><a href="#cite_note-329">[327]</a></sup> Since the establishment of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/J._League" title="J. League">Japan Professional Football League</a> in 1992, association football has gained a wide following.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-330"><a href="#cite_note-330">[328]</a></sup> The country co-hosted the <a href="/wiki/2002_FIFA_World_Cup" title="2002 FIFA World Cup">2002 FIFA World Cup</a> with South Korea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-331"><a href="#cite_note-331">[329]</a></sup> Japan has one of the most successful football teams in Asia, winning the <a href="/wiki/AFC_Asian_Cup" title="AFC Asian Cup">Asian Cup</a> four times,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-332"><a href="#cite_note-332">[330]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/2011_FIFA_Women%27s_World_Cup" title="2011 FIFA Women's World Cup">FIFA Women's World Cup</a> in 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-333"><a href="#cite_note-333">[331]</a></sup> Golf is also popular in Japan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-334"><a href="#cite_note-334">[332]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Motorsport" title="Motorsport">motorsport</a>, Japanese automotive manufacturers have been successful in multiple different categories, with titles and victories in series such as <a href="/wiki/Formula_One" title="Formula One">Formula One</a>, <a href="/wiki/Grand_Prix_motorcycle_racing" title="Grand Prix motorcycle racing">MotoGP</a>, <a href="/wiki/IndyCar_Series" title="IndyCar Series">IndyCar</a>, <a href="/wiki/World_Rally_Championship" title="World Rally Championship">World Rally Championship</a>, <a href="/wiki/FIA_World_Endurance_Championship" title="FIA World Endurance Championship">World Endurance Championship</a>, <a href="/wiki/World_Touring_Car_Championship" title="World Touring Car Championship">World Touring Car Championship</a>, <a href="/wiki/British_Touring_Car_Championship" title="British Touring Car Championship">British Touring Car Championship</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/WeatherTech_SportsCar_Championship" title="WeatherTech SportsCar Championship">IMSA SportsCar Championship</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-335"><a href="#cite_note-335">[333]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-336"><a href="#cite_note-336">[334]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-337"><a href="#cite_note-337">[335]</a></sup> Three Japanese drivers have achieved podium finishes in Formula One, and drivers from Japan have victories at the <a href="/wiki/Indianapolis_500" title="Indianapolis 500">Indianapolis 500</a> and the <a href="/wiki/24_Hours_of_Le_Mans" title="24 Hours of Le Mans">24 Hours of Le Mans</a>, in addition to success in domestic championships.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-338"><a href="#cite_note-338">[336]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-339"><a href="#cite_note-339">[337]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Super_GT" title="Super GT">Super GT</a> is the most popular national series in Japan, while <a href="/wiki/Super_Formula_Championship" title="Super Formula Championship">Super Formula</a> is the top level domestic open-wheel series.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-340"><a href="#cite_note-340">[338]</a></sup> The country hosts major races such as the <a href="/wiki/Japanese_Grand_Prix" title="Japanese Grand Prix">Japanese Grand Prix</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-341"><a href="#cite_note-341">[339]</a></sup>
</p><p>Japan hosted the Summer Olympics in <a href="/wiki/1964_Summer_Olympics" title="1964 Summer Olympics">Tokyo in 1964</a> and the Winter Olympics in <a href="/wiki/1972_Winter_Olympics" title="1972 Winter Olympics">Sapporo in 1972</a> and <a href="/wiki/1998_Winter_Olympics" title="1998 Winter Olympics">Nagano in 1998</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-342"><a href="#cite_note-342">[340]</a></sup> The country hosted the official <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2006_Basketball_World_Championship" title="2006 Basketball World Championship">2006 Basketball World Championship</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-343"><a href="#cite_note-343">[341]</a></sup> and will co-host the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2023_Basketball_World_Championship" title="2023 Basketball World Championship">2023 Basketball World Championship</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-344"><a href="#cite_note-344">[342]</a></sup> Tokyo hosted the <a href="/wiki/2020_Summer_Olympics" title="2020 Summer Olympics">2020 Summer Olympics</a> in 2021, making Tokyo the first Asian city to host the Olympics twice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-345"><a href="#cite_note-345">[343]</a></sup> The country gained the hosting rights for the official <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Women%27s_Volleyball_World_Championship" title="Women's Volleyball World Championship">Women's Volleyball World Championship</a> on five occasions, more than any other nation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-346"><a href="#cite_note-346">[344]</a></sup> Japan is the most successful Asian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rugby_Union" title="Rugby Union">Rugby Union</a> country<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-347"><a href="#cite_note-347">[345]</a></sup> and hosted the 2019 IRB <a href="/wiki/Rugby_World_Cup" title="Rugby World Cup">Rugby World Cup</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-348"><a href="#cite_note-348">[346]</a></sup>
</p>

<li id="cite_note-fn1-10"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-fn1_10-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In English, the official name of the country is simply "Japan".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[8]</a></sup> In Japanese, the name of the country as it appears on official documents, including the <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Japan" title="Constitution of Japan">country's constitution</a>, is <span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">日本国</span></span> (<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap;margin-right:.25em;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ja-nippon_nihonkoku.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="" data-file-height="20" data-file-width="20" decoding="async" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" width="11"/></a></span><a class="internal" href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/86/Ja-nippon_nihonkoku.ogg" title="Ja-nippon nihonkoku.ogg"><span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">Nippon-koku</i></span></a></span></span> or <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">Nihon-koku</i></span>), meaning "State of Japan". Despite this, the short-form name <span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">日本</span></span> (<span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">Nippon</i></span> or <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">Nihon</i></span>) is also often used officially.</span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>
